    Lillian Dickson (Â≠´ÁêÜËìÆ, 1901.1.29 ‚Äì 1983.1.14)

    Lillian R. Dickson was anAmerican missionary, social worker, and humanitarian who played a key role inChristian missionary work and social welfare development in Taiwan, particularly amongindigenous communities, leprosy patients, and those in need of medical care and vocational training.

    Born onJanuary 29, 1901, inPelican Lake, Minnesota, USA, she attendedMacalester College. After graduating in 1924, her future husband,James Dickson, continued his studies atPrinceton Theological Seminary, while Lillian worked as a high school teacher before enrolling at theNew York Bible Theological Seminary (Liu Huihua, 1987).

    In1927, the newlywedJames and Lillian Dickson were sent asmissionaries by the Presbyterian Church in Canada. They traveled across the Pacific by ship, landing in Shanghai before making their way toKeelung, Taiwan, where they began theirmissionary journey‚Äîahoneymoon that became a lifelong calling (Su Guangyang, 1978).

    Early Years in Taiwan (1927-1941)

    The couple settled inTamsui in1929, whereRev. James Dickson served asprincipal of Tamsui Middle School and laterpresident of Taiwan Theological Seminary. He alsopreached among indigenous communities in the mountains.

    During this period, Lillian primarily took on therole of a wife and mother, raising their three children. However,World War II forced theDickson family to leave Taiwan due to their American nationality. They were reassigned toBritish Guiana (Guyana), South America, where they continued missionary work, gaining experience inSunday school programs and outdoor evangelism‚Äîpreparing Lillian for herreturn to Taiwan after the war (Tank, 1986).

    Post-War Return and Missionary Expansion (1947-1950s)

    InApril 1947, James and Lillian Dickson returned to Taiwan, with Jamesfocusing on theological education andindigenous evangelism. He spentweekdays teaching at the seminary andweekends driving a jeep into remote mountain areas to spread the gospel.Lillian accompanied him, dedicating herself towomen‚Äôs and children‚Äôs ministries (Li Zhende, 2005: 98, 111).

    She once told her husband:

    üëâ‚ÄúI don‚Äôt want to be just a missionary‚Äôs wife; I want to be a missionary wife.‚Äù

    To which he replied:

    üëâ‚ÄúYou can do things informally that the rest of us cannot.‚Äù

    During this period,many indigenous churches in Taiwan were established, largely due to the couple‚Äôsmissionary efforts. However, Lillian sawan even greater need in Taiwanese society and sooninitiated her own ministries (Su Guangyang, 1978).

    Social Welfare and Medical Ministries (1951-1960s)

    -1951 ‚Äì Lillian began publishingmonthly newsletters to update supporters in Canada and the U.S. She signed them with‚ÄùYours in His service‚Äù, emphasizing that donors werepartners in the ministry rather than mere financial contributors (Dickson, 2016). At her peak, she sent5,000 airmail and 20,000 regular mail letters per month.

    -1952 ‚Äì Responding to localpastors‚Äô calls for help, she beganministering at Lo-Sheng Leprosarium (Ê®ÇÁîüÁôÇÈ§äÈô¢) in Xinzhuang,providing medical, spiritual, and social care for leprosy patients. That year, shehelped establish Sheng-Wang Church (ËÅñÊúõÊïôÊúÉ) inside the leprosarium and continuedworshiping with patients until her death (Liu Huihua, 1987).

    -1954 ‚Äì She formally founded theMustard Seed Mission (Ëä•ËèúÁ®ÆÊúÉ), now known as theMustard Seed Christian Mission Foundation, tosupport social welfare efforts, funded primarily bydonors from the U.S. and Canada (Liu Huihua, 1987).

    -1956 ‚Äì Concerned abouttuberculosis among indigenous children, she establishedadult and children‚Äôs TB sanatoriums, which later led to the founding ofPuli Christian Hospital.

    -1960 ‚Äì InBeimen Township, Tainan, she established the‚ÄùGate of Mercy‚Äù clinic forblackfoot disease patients. TheMustard Seed Mission also providedmilk, rice, flour, and clothing for those in need (Dickson, 2016).

    -1961 ‚Äì She establishedmaternity wards for indigenous mothers, starting inHualien, and later expanded them across Taiwan. These maternity centers helpeddeliver approximately 240 babies per month until1975 (Tank, 1986).

    Vocational Training and Church Building (1958-1970s)

    -1958 ‚Äì TheMustard Seed Mission established vocational training centers in Hualien for indigenous youth, training over20,000 graduates in various trades. Nearlyhalf of the early indigenous pastors in thePresbyterian Church in Taiwan were alumni of these programs (Huang Wu-dong, 1954).

    -1963 ‚Äì She launched an initiative encouragingindigenous Christians to build stone churches, usinglocally sourced stone whilecement was funded by North American donors (Tank, 1986).

    International Missions and Final Years (1967-1983)

    -1967 ‚Äì At age66, Lillianexpanded her mission work overseas, founding theBurning Bush Mission (ÁÑöÊ£òÊµ∑Â§ñÂÆ£ÈÅìÊúÉ). She partnered with thePresbyterian Church in Taiwan, sendingeight indigenous pastors toSarawak, Malaysia, for mission work.

    Lillian Dicksonpassed away on January 14, 1983, after51 years of missionary, charitable, and educational service in Taiwan. She diedwhile actively working and wasburied alongside her husband at Taiwan Theological Seminary inLingtou, Taipei (Su Guangyang, 1978).

    Legacy

    Lillian Dickson wasone of the most influential missionaries in Taiwan‚Äôs history. Her workbridged evangelism and social action, addressingthe physical, educational, and spiritual needs of Taiwanese communities‚Äîespeciallyindigenous groups, leprosy patients, and the impoverished. Through herMustard Seed Mission, sheempowered thousands throughmedical care, education, and vocational training, shapingTaiwan‚Äôs Christian welfare landscape for generations.

      ËÄåÂ≠´ÈõÖÂêÑÊúÄÁÇ∫Áü•ÂêçÁöÑÔºåÂ∞±ÊòØÂú®ÈáçËøîÂè∞ÁÅ£ÂæåÊé®ÂãïÂè∞ÁÅ£Âéü‰ΩèÊ∞ëÂÆ£ÊïôÔºå1946Âπ¥6Êúà‰ªñÂ∞±Âà∞Ëä±ËìÆÂ∑°Ë¶ñÂ±±Âú∞ÊïôÊúÉÔºåÈñãÂßã‰ªñÁöÑÂéü‰ΩèÊ∞ëÂÆ£ÊïôÂ∑•‰Ωú„ÄÇÊï∏Âπ¥Èñì‰ªñÈÄèÈÅéÂúãÂÖßÂ§ñÁ±åÊ¨æÔºåÂçîÂä©‰∫Ü385ÊâÄÊïôÊúÉÂæûÁ´πÂª∫ÊïôÂ†ÇÊîπÂª∫ÊàñÊñ∞Âª∫ÁÇ∫Áü≥È†≠Ê∞¥Ê≥•ÊïôÂ†Ç„ÄÇ‰∏¶Âú®ÁéâÁ•ûËçâÂâµÁ∂ìË≤ªÊãÆÊçÆÁöÑÂàùÊúüÔºåËàáÈªÉÂΩ∞ËºùÈô¢Èï∑Á©çÊ•µÂêëÊôÆ‰∏ñÊïôÊúÉÁ±åÊé™Á∂ìË≤ªÔºå‰øÉÊàêÁéâÂ±±Á•ûÂ≠∏Èô¢ÁöÑÂâµÁ´ã„ÄÇ‰ªñ‰πüÈï∑ÊúüÈÄèÈÅéÂè∞ÁÅ£Âü∫Áù£Èï∑ËÄÅÊïôÊúÉÁöÑË≥áÊ∫êÔºåÂçîÂä©Â¶ªÂ≠êÂ≠´ÁêÜËìÆÂú®Âè∞ÁöÑÁ§æÊúÉÁ¶èÂà©Â∑•‰ΩúÔºåÂ¶ÇÂè∞ÁÅ£Êù±ÈÉ®ÂèäË•øÈÉ®ÁöÑÂ±±ÂçÄÈñãÂ±ïÈÜ´ÁôÇÂíåÊïëÊøüÁöÑÂ∑•‰Ωú„ÄÇÂ≠´ÈõÖÂêÑÁ∏ΩÊòØÈÅãÁî®ÂêÑÁ®ÆÁÆ°ÈÅìÁà≠ÂèñÊïôËÇ≤Ë≥áÊ∫êÂèäÂêà‰ΩúÂ∞çË±°ÔºåÂ¶ÇÂú®Êà∞ÂæåÂ§ßÈáèËΩâÈÄ≤Âè∞ÁÅ£ÁöÑÂ∑ÆÊúÉ‰∫∫ÂäõËàáË≤°ÂäõÂΩ¢Êàê„ÄåÂúãË™ûÊïôÊúÉ„ÄçÊÄ•ÈÄüÊì¥Â±ïÁöÑÁãÄÊ≥ÅÔºå‰ªñ‰πüÂú®‰∏≠ÈñìÊâÆÊºîÈõôÊñπÁöÑÊΩ§ÊªëÂäëÂíåÊï¥ÂêàÂäõÈáè„ÄÇ

      1967Âπ¥Â≠´ÈõÖÂêÑÂú®ÁæéÂúãÊ™¢È©óÂá∫ËÖ¶Áò§ÔºåÈö®Âç≥ËøîÂõûÊïÖÈÑâÂè∞ÁÅ£ÔºåÂêåÂπ¥6Êúà‰∏≠ÁóÖÈÄùÂ∂∫È†≠ÂÆ∂‰∏≠ÔºåËë¨ÊñºÂè∞ÁÅ£Á•ûÂ≠∏Èô¢„ÄÇËÄåÂ≠´ÁêÜËìÆÁπºÊâø‰ªñÁöÑÈÅ∫ÂøóÔºåÂú®ÈöîÂπ¥ÊàêÁ´ãÁÑöÊ£òÊµ∑Â§ñÂÆ£ÈÅìÊúÉÔºåÈ¶ñÂ∫¶Ê¥æÈÅ£Âè∞ÁÅ£Âéü‰ΩèÊ∞ëËá≥ÂçóÊ¥ãÈ¶¨‰æÜË•ø‰∫ûÁ†ÇÊãâË∂äÁ≠âÂú∞ÂÆ£Êïô„ÄÇÔºàÂè∞ÁÅ£Âü∫Áù£Èï∑ËÄÅÊïéÊúÉÁ∏ΩÊúÉÂéü‰ΩèÊ∞ëÂÆ£ÈÅìÂßîÂì°ÊúÉÔºå1998Ôºå297Ôºâ

      Rev. James Ira Dickson (Â≠´ÈõÖÂêÑ, 1900.2.23 ‚Äì 1967.6.15)

      James Ira Dickson was anAmerican Presbyterian missionary, theologian, and educator who played apivotal role in theological education and indigenous missions in Taiwan. His work significantly impacted theTaiwan Theological Seminary, indigenous church development, and social welfare initiatives.

      Early Life and Arrival in Taiwan (1927-1940)

      Born inSouth Dakota, USA,Dickson graduated from Princeton Theological Seminary in 1927. That same year, he marriedLillian Dickson (Â≠´ÁêÜËìÆ).

      During this period, theUnited Church of Canada movement causeddivisions within the Canadian Presbyterian Church, leading toa shortage of missionaries. Although he was American,Dickson was deeply influenced by the Student Volunteer Movement for Foreign Missions. InOctober 1927, he accepted amissionary appointment from the Presbyterian Church in Canada and was sent toTaiwan.

      In Taiwan, Dickson served invarious leadership roles:

      -Acting Principal of Tamsui Middle School (Ê∑°Ê∞¥‰∏≠Â≠∏)

      -President of Taipei Theological Seminary (now Taiwan Theological Seminary, Âè∞ÁÅ£Á•ûÂ≠∏Èô¢)

      -President of the Presbyterian Church in Taiwan Bible College

      However,in 1940, during thefinal years of World War II,the Japanese Governor-General‚Äôs Office pressured American missionaries to leave Taiwan. The Dickson family relocated toBritish Guiana (Guyana), South America, where they continuedmissionary work and theological education (Deng Xiangyang, 2003: 82).

      Post-War Return and Indigenous Missions (1946-1960s)

      After World War II, the Dicksonsreturned to Taiwan in 1946. That same year, James Dicksontraveled to Shanghai to invite Mennonite medical missionaries to serve Taiwan‚Äôs indigenous communities. As a result, in1947, theMennonite Central Committee (MCC) dispatched doctors and nurses, forming theMennonite Mountain Medical Team (Huang Wenjiang, Zhang Yunkai, Chen Zhiheng, 2015: 346).

      In1948, Dickson resumed his role asPresident of Taiwan Theological Seminary, where he led significant institutional changes. His mostcontroversial yet impactful decision was torelocate the seminary from Taipei‚Äôs Shuanglian district to Lingtou, Yangmingshan in1955, despite opposition. Over the nextten years, he oversaw the development of the new campus, completing the facilities by1965, before retiring.

      Church Renewal and Indigenous Evangelism

      Beyond theological education, Dickson was akey figure in Taiwan‚Äôs church renewal movement, particularly among young Presbyterian leaders in northern Taiwan.

      Hismost renowned contribution was hisdedication to indigenous missionary work.

      • InJune 1946, he visitedHualien‚Äôs mountain churches, marking the beginning ofhis deep engagement with indigenous evangelism.

        • Over the years, throughfundraising from local and international sources, he helpedbuild or renovate 385 churches, transforming many frombamboo structures to stone and concrete buildings.

          Establishment of Yushan Theological Seminary (ÁéâÂ±±Á•ûÂ≠∏Èô¢)

          During theearly years of Yushan Theological Seminary,financial constraints threatened its survival.Dickson and President Huang Zhanghui (ÈªÉÂΩ∞Ëºù) activelysecured international funding, ultimately ensuring the seminary‚Äôs establishment as a center forindigenous theological education.

          Support for Social Welfare and Medical Missions

          ThroughPresbyterian Church resources, Dicksonsupported his wife, Lillian Dickson, in hersocial welfare work, including:

          -Medical and relief programs in Taiwan‚Äôs mountain regions

          -Expansion of healthcare access in indigenous communities

          During thepost-war period, asurge in foreign missionaries and resources led to therapid growth of Mandarin-speaking churches (ÂúãË™ûÊïôÊúÉ). Dickson played acrucial mediating role, helpingintegrate foreign mission efforts with local church initiatives.

          Final Years and Legacy

          In1967, while in the United States,Dickson was diagnosed with a brain tumor. Heimmediately returned to Taiwan, where hepassed away in June at his home in Lingtou. He was buried atTaiwan Theological Seminary, where he had devoted much of his life to theological education.

          After his passing,Lillian Dickson carried on his mission work. In1968, she founded theBurning Bush Mission (ÁÑöÊ£òÊµ∑Â§ñÂÆ£ÈÅìÊúÉ), which sentTaiwanese indigenous missionaries toSarawak, Malaysia, marking the firstoverseas indigenous mission initiative from Taiwan (Indigenous Missions Committee of the Presbyterian Church in Taiwan, 1998: 297).

          Conclusion

          James Dickson was atransformational leader in Taiwan‚Äôs Christian history. Hispioneering work in theological education, indigenous evangelism, and church renewal left alasting legacy. Through hisinstitution-building, missionary efforts, and advocacy for indigenous ministry, he helpedshape Taiwan‚Äôs Presbyterian Church and strengthen its global connections.

          Ê•äÁæéÈΩãÁâßÂ∏´1949Âπ¥‰æÜÂà∞Âè∞ÁÅ£È¶ñÂÖàÂª∫Á´ãÂü∫Áù£ÊïôÊµ∏‰ø°ÊúÉ‰ªÅÊÑõÂ†ÇÔºåÁÇ∫Êµ∏‰ø°ÊúÉÂú®Âè∞ÁÅ£Á¨¨‰∏ÄÊâÄÊïôÊúÉÔºåÊ•äÁæéÈΩãÁâßÂ∏´Âú®ÁâßÊúÉÊúüÈñìÁúãË¶ãÂè∞ÁÅ£Á¶èÈü≥Â∑•‰ΩúÁöÑÈúÄË¶ÅÔºå‰∏¶Á©çÊ•µÈñãÊãìÂª∫Á´ãÁ¶èÈü≥‰∏≠ÂøÉÔºåÂ¶Ç‰ªäÂùáÂ∑≤ÊàêÁÇ∫Êµ∏‰ø°ÊúÉÊ≠∑Âè≤ÊÇ†‰πÖÁöÑÊïôÊúÉÔºåÂ¶ÇÊñ∞Á´πÊ®πÊûóÈ†≠ÈñãÂßãÂÆ∂Â∫≠ËÅöÊúÉÔºà1951Âπ¥12Êúà16Êó•Êñ∞Á´πÊµ∏‰ø°ÊúÉÊàêÁ´ãÔºâ„ÄÅÂªàÈñÄË°óÁ¶èÈü≥ÊâÄ„ÄÇ1952Âπ¥ÔºåÁæéÂçóÊµ∏‰ø°ÊúÉÂ∑ÆÊúÉÂú®Ëá∫ÁÅ£ÈñãËæ¶Âü∫Áù£ÊïôÂè∞ÁÅ£Êµ∏ÊúÉÁ•ûÂ≠∏Èô¢ÔºåÊüØÁêÜÂüπÁâßÂ∏´(Charles L. Culpepper, 1895-1986)ÁÇ∫Á¨¨‰∏Ä‰ªªÈô¢Èï∑ÔºõÂàùÊúüÂ∏´Ë≥á‰∏çË∂≥ÊôÇÔºåÊ•äÁæéÈΩãÊäïÂÖ•ËàäÁ¥ÑËÅñÁ∂ìÊïôÊéàÂ∑•‰ΩúÔºåÂçîÂä©ÂüπËÇ≤Ëá∫ÁÅ£Áï∂Âú∞ÁöÑÂÇ≥Êïô‰∫∫Êâç„ÄÇÊüØÁêÜÂüπÈô¢Èï∑„ÄÅ1948Âπ¥10Êúà‰æÜÂè∞ÂÆ£ÊïôÁöÑÁæéÂçóÊµ∏‰ø°ÊúÉÊòé‰øäÂæ∑ÂÆ£ÊïôÂ£´ÔºàMiss Bertha SmithÔºâÂèäÊ•äÁæéÈΩãÁâßÂ∏´‰∏â‰ΩçÂç≥ÁÇ∫Êó©ÊúüËá∫ÁÅ£Êµ∏‰ø°ÊúÉÈáçË¶ÅÊ≠∑Âè≤‰∫∫Áâ©„ÄÇÔºàÂê≥Êñá‰ªÅÔºå1998Ôºå49„ÄÅ50„ÄÅ233Ôºâ

          Rev. Vang Mei-Chai (Ê•äÁæéÈΩã, 1889.9.18 ‚Äì 1961.2.23)

          Rev.Vang Mei-Chai was aChinese Baptist missionary and church planter, recognized as thepioneer of Baptist ministry in Taiwan. His efforts were instrumental inestablishing the Baptist Church in Taiwan andtraining local Christian leaders.

          Early Life and Ministry in China (1889-1948)

          Born inMaguo Village, Shandong, China,Vang Mei-Chai received atraditional education in village schools before entering thePingdu Baptist Mission Middle School at age25. In1913, he enrolled at theBaptist Theological Seminary in Huang County, Shandong, graduating in1916.

          After graduation, heserved as a missionary inNortheastern China and Shandong. Beginning in1920, he spenttwelve years establishingtwelve churches acrossHarbin, Anda, Lindian, Mingshui, Ang‚Äôangxi, Qiqihar, Fulaerji, Zalantun, Jiamusi, Suifenhe, Boketu, and Manzhouli. His pioneering efforts helpedexpand Baptist mission work in Manchuria.

          In1936, at theCentennial Celebration of Baptist Missions in China in Guangzhou, he became an advocate forfrontier evangelism. The following year, during theFirst National Evangelistic Conference,21 pastors committed to frontier missions, includingVang Mei-Chai.

          In1948, theChinese Baptist Convention convened afrontier missions conference in Shanghai, where it was decided tosend Rev. Vang Mei-Chai to Taiwan to begin Baptist work. After aone-month exploratory trip, he returned to Shanghai and receivedofficial approval to startBaptist missions in Taiwan.

          Pioneering Baptist Missions in Taiwan (1949-1961)

          OnFebruary 4, 1949,Vang Mei-Chai arrived in Taiwan, landing inKeelung with his family. Over the next13 years, helaid the foundation for the Baptist Church in Taiwan, earning him the title‚ÄùPioneer of Baptist Ministry in Taiwan‚Äù.

          -1949 ‚Äì Established theRen-Ai Baptist Church (‰ªÅÊÑõÂ†Ç), thefirst Baptist church in Taiwan.

          -1951 ‚Äì Initiatedhouse church gatherings inShulin, Hsinchu, which later developed intoHsinchu Baptist Church (established onDecember 16, 1951).

          -1951 ‚Äì Opened theXiamen Street Gospel Center in Taipei.

          -1952 ‚Äì Played a key role in the founding of theTaiwan Baptist Theological Seminary (Âè∞ÁÅ£Êµ∏‰ø°ÊúÉÁ•ûÂ≠∏Èô¢), established by theSouthern Baptist Mission in Taiwan.

          • The seminary‚Äôs first president wasRev. Charles L. Culpepper (ÊüØÁêÜÂüπ, 1895-1986).

            • Due to ashortage of faculty,Vang Mei-Chai served as aprofessor of Old Testament, training Taiwan‚Äôs early Baptist pastors.

              • Along withRev. Culpepper andMiss Bertha Smith (Êòé‰øäÂæ∑, arrived in 1948), he became one of thethree most influential early figures in Taiwan‚Äôs Baptist history (Wu Wenren, 1998: 49, 50, 233).

                Final Years and Legacy

                OnFebruary 23, 1961,Vang Mei-Chai passed away from liver disease and was buried in theBitan Mountain area of Xindian, New Taipei City.

                At hismemorial service,Rev. Charles Culpepper honored him with the words:

                üëâ‚ÄúA faithful servant of the Lord throughout his life.‚Äù

                Significance in Taiwan‚Äôs Christian History

                Rev. Vang Mei-Chai‚Äôs contributions toTaiwan‚Äôs Baptist movement were foundational. He:

                -Planted Taiwan‚Äôs first Baptist churches.

                -Trained local pastors and church leaders.

                -Strengthened theological education throughTaiwan Baptist Theological Seminary.

                Hisevangelistic zeal, leadership, and dedication helpedestablish a lasting Baptist presence in Taiwan, shaping thegrowth of Baptist Christianity on the island.

                Witness Lee (ÊùéÂ∏∏Âèó, 1905.9.3 ‚Äì 1997.6.9)

                Witness Lee was aChinese Christian leader, preacher, and theologian, known for his leadership in theLocal Church Movement (Âè¨ÊúÉ/Âú∞ÊñπÂè¨ÊúÉ), as well as forestablishing Living Stream Ministry (Ê∞¥ÊµÅËÅ∑‰∫ãÁ´ô). As thesuccessor to Watchman Nee (ÂÄ™ÊüùËÅ≤), he played akey role in expanding the movement across China, Taiwan, and the United States.

                Early Life and Ministry in China (1905-1949)

                Born inNorth China, Lee was raised in aChristian family, with his mother being a devoted believer. Hisearly education was sporadic, but he attendedAmerican Presbyterian Church‚Äôs Yiwen Business School (ÁæéÂåóÈï∑ËÄÅÊïôÊúÉÁõäÊñáÂïÜÂ∞à), where hecommitted to full-time ministry at the age of 19.

                After encounteringWatchman Nee (ÂÄ™ÊüùËÅ≤), Lee became deeply influenced by his teachings. He worked as amissionary in Yantai and Shanghai, establishingLocal Churches in Northern China, including in his hometown ofYantai, Shandong (Lee, 2005: 48-50).

                In1934, Lee moved toShanghai, where he beganworking closely with Watchman Nee. That same year, Neeentrusted him with leading the Shanghai Gospel Book Room (‰∏äÊµ∑Á¶èÈü≥Êõ∏Êàø), the publishing branch of the movement. Over the next15 years, he ministered inNanjing, Shanghai, South China, and beyond, planting and strengthening Local Churches across China (Lee, 2005: 181-182).

                By1949, as theChinese Communist Party took control of China, Watchman Nee and other leaders sentLee to Taiwan to continue the work (Lee, 2005: 227-230).

                Establishing the Local Church in Taiwan (1949-1962)

                Arriving in Taiwan with only350 believers, Lee oversawrapid church growth‚Äîwithinfive years, membershipgrew to 20,000, leading to the global expansion of theLocal Church Movement.

                Key developments during this period included:

                -Founding the Taiwan Gospel Book Room (Âè∞ÁÅ£Á¶èÈü≥Êõ∏Êàø) to publish Christian literature.

                • ExpandingLocal Churches (Âú∞ÊñπÂè¨ÊúÉ) throughout Taiwan.

                  Doctrinal Disputes and Church Splits (1957-1965)

                  In1957, Lee had atheological disagreement withTheodore Austin-Sparks (Âè≤ÁôæÂÖã, 1888-1971), a leader of theInner Life Movement, regardingchurch structure and authority. This led to asplit in the Local Church in Taiwan (Lee, 2005: 32).

                  Further challenges to his leadership emerged:

                  -1961 ‚Äì TheManila Local Church split, questioning his authority.

                  -1965 ‚Äì Disputes overLee‚Äôs leadership led to furthercontroversies in Hong Kong (Lee, 2005: 84-88).

                  Expansion to the United States and Global Influence (1962-1997)

                  In1962, Lee moved toLos Angeles, USA, launching a35-year evangelical mission.

                  -1965 ‚Äì He foundedLiving Stream Ministry (Ê∞¥ÊµÅËÅ∑‰∫ãÁ´ô) inAnaheim, California, becoming the official publishing house forNee and Lee‚Äôs teachings.

                  -1965-1987 ‚Äì Leespent 22 years writing Life-Study of the Bible (ÁîüÂëΩËÆÄÁ∂ì), acomprehensive biblical commentary spanning 25,000+ pages in 89 volumes and 1,984 messages.

                  -1987 ‚Äì He completed theChinese Recovery Version of the New Testament (ÊÅ¢Âæ©Êú¨ËÅñÁ∂ì) and supervised itsEnglish translation.

                  Later Years and Controversies (1987-1997)

                  In his later years, Leereturned to Taiwan to promote the‚ÄùNew Way‚Äù (Êñ∞Ë∑ØÈÅãÂãï, also called ‚ÄúGod‚Äôs Ordained Way‚Äù/Á•ûÂëΩÂÆö‰πãË∑Ø), aiming torestructure the church and institutionalize leadership training. However, this movement facedresistance, particularly regardinghis handling of moral issues involving his son, Lee Meng-ze (ÊùéËíôÊæ§).

                  Despite these challenges, Lee continuedpreaching and writing. Even at91 years old (February 1997), he delivered public sermons atspecial conferences.

                  OnJune 9, 1997,Witness Lee passed away in Anaheim, California, at the age of 92.

                  Legacy

                  Lee left a lasting impact onChinese Christianity, particularly through:

                  1.The Global Expansion of the Local Church Movement

                  2.The Establishment of Living Stream Ministry

                  3.His Extensive Biblical Commentaries and Doctrinal Teachings

                  Despitetheological controversies and organizational disputes, his work continues toshape evangelical movements in China, Taiwan, and beyond.

Key Theological Issues

    Lillian Dickson (Â≠´ÁêÜËìÆ, 1901.1.29 ‚Äì 1983.1.14)

    Lillian R. Dickson was anAmerican missionary, social worker, and humanitarian who played a key role inChristian missionary work and social welfare development in Taiwan, particularly amongindigenous communities, leprosy patients, and those in need of medical care and vocational training.

    Born onJanuary 29, 1901, inPelican Lake, Minnesota, USA, she attendedMacalester College. After graduating in 1924, her future husband,James Dickson, continued his studies atPrinceton Theological Seminary, while Lillian worked as a high school teacher before enrolling at theNew York Bible Theological Seminary (Liu Huihua, 1987).

    In1927, the newlywedJames and Lillian Dickson were sent asmissionaries by the Presbyterian Church in Canada. They traveled across the Pacific by ship, landing in Shanghai before making their way toKeelung, Taiwan, where they began theirmissionary journey‚Äîahoneymoon that became a lifelong calling (Su Guangyang, 1978).

    Early Years in Taiwan (1927-1941)

    The couple settled inTamsui in1929, whereRev. James Dickson served asprincipal of Tamsui Middle School and laterpresident of Taiwan Theological Seminary. He alsopreached among indigenous communities in the mountains.

    During this period, Lillian primarily took on therole of a wife and mother, raising their three children. However,World War II forced theDickson family to leave Taiwan due to their American nationality. They were reassigned toBritish Guiana (Guyana), South America, where they continued missionary work, gaining experience inSunday school programs and outdoor evangelism‚Äîpreparing Lillian for herreturn to Taiwan after the war (Tank, 1986).

    Post-War Return and Missionary Expansion (1947-1950s)

    InApril 1947, James and Lillian Dickson returned to Taiwan, with Jamesfocusing on theological education andindigenous evangelism. He spentweekdays teaching at the seminary andweekends driving a jeep into remote mountain areas to spread the gospel.Lillian accompanied him, dedicating herself towomen‚Äôs and children‚Äôs ministries (Li Zhende, 2005: 98, 111).

    She once told her husband:

    üëâ‚ÄúI don‚Äôt want to be just a missionary‚Äôs wife; I want to be a missionary wife.‚Äù

    To which he replied:

    üëâ‚ÄúYou can do things informally that the rest of us cannot.‚Äù

    During this period,many indigenous churches in Taiwan were established, largely due to the couple‚Äôsmissionary efforts. However, Lillian sawan even greater need in Taiwanese society and sooninitiated her own ministries (Su Guangyang, 1978).

    Social Welfare and Medical Ministries (1951-1960s)

    -1951 ‚Äì Lillian began publishingmonthly newsletters to update supporters in Canada and the U.S. She signed them with‚ÄùYours in His service‚Äù, emphasizing that donors werepartners in the ministry rather than mere financial contributors (Dickson, 2016). At her peak, she sent5,000 airmail and 20,000 regular mail letters per month.

    -1952 ‚Äì Responding to localpastors‚Äô calls for help, she beganministering at Lo-Sheng Leprosarium (Ê®ÇÁîüÁôÇÈ§äÈô¢) in Xinzhuang,providing medical, spiritual, and social care for leprosy patients. That year, shehelped establish Sheng-Wang Church (ËÅñÊúõÊïôÊúÉ) inside the leprosarium and continuedworshiping with patients until her death (Liu Huihua, 1987).

    -1954 ‚Äì She formally founded theMustard Seed Mission (Ëä•ËèúÁ®ÆÊúÉ), now known as theMustard Seed Christian Mission Foundation, tosupport social welfare efforts, funded primarily bydonors from the U.S. and Canada (Liu Huihua, 1987).

    -1956 ‚Äì Concerned abouttuberculosis among indigenous children, she establishedadult and children‚Äôs TB sanatoriums, which later led to the founding ofPuli Christian Hospital.

    -1960 ‚Äì InBeimen Township, Tainan, she established the‚ÄùGate of Mercy‚Äù clinic forblackfoot disease patients. TheMustard Seed Mission also providedmilk, rice, flour, and clothing for those in need (Dickson, 2016).

    -1961 ‚Äì She establishedmaternity wards for indigenous mothers, starting inHualien, and later expanded them across Taiwan. These maternity centers helpeddeliver approximately 240 babies per month until1975 (Tank, 1986).

    Vocational Training and Church Building (1958-1970s)

    -1958 ‚Äì TheMustard Seed Mission established vocational training centers in Hualien for indigenous youth, training over20,000 graduates in various trades. Nearlyhalf of the early indigenous pastors in thePresbyterian Church in Taiwan were alumni of these programs (Huang Wu-dong, 1954).

    -1963 ‚Äì She launched an initiative encouragingindigenous Christians to build stone churches, usinglocally sourced stone whilecement was funded by North American donors (Tank, 1986).

    International Missions and Final Years (1967-1983)

    -1967 ‚Äì At age66, Lillianexpanded her mission work overseas, founding theBurning Bush Mission (ÁÑöÊ£òÊµ∑Â§ñÂÆ£ÈÅìÊúÉ). She partnered with thePresbyterian Church in Taiwan, sendingeight indigenous pastors toSarawak, Malaysia, for mission work.

    Lillian Dicksonpassed away on January 14, 1983, after51 years of missionary, charitable, and educational service in Taiwan. She diedwhile actively working and wasburied alongside her husband at Taiwan Theological Seminary inLingtou, Taipei (Su Guangyang, 1978).

    Legacy

    Lillian Dickson wasone of the most influential missionaries in Taiwan‚Äôs history. Her workbridged evangelism and social action, addressingthe physical, educational, and spiritual needs of Taiwanese communities‚Äîespeciallyindigenous groups, leprosy patients, and the impoverished. Through herMustard Seed Mission, sheempowered thousands throughmedical care, education, and vocational training, shapingTaiwan‚Äôs Christian welfare landscape for generations.

      ËÄåÂ≠´ÈõÖÂêÑÊúÄÁÇ∫Áü•ÂêçÁöÑÔºåÂ∞±ÊòØÂú®ÈáçËøîÂè∞ÁÅ£ÂæåÊé®ÂãïÂè∞ÁÅ£Âéü‰ΩèÊ∞ëÂÆ£ÊïôÔºå1946Âπ¥6Êúà‰ªñÂ∞±Âà∞Ëä±ËìÆÂ∑°Ë¶ñÂ±±Âú∞ÊïôÊúÉÔºåÈñãÂßã‰ªñÁöÑÂéü‰ΩèÊ∞ëÂÆ£ÊïôÂ∑•‰Ωú„ÄÇÊï∏Âπ¥Èñì‰ªñÈÄèÈÅéÂúãÂÖßÂ§ñÁ±åÊ¨æÔºåÂçîÂä©‰∫Ü385ÊâÄÊïôÊúÉÂæûÁ´πÂª∫ÊïôÂ†ÇÊîπÂª∫ÊàñÊñ∞Âª∫ÁÇ∫Áü≥È†≠Ê∞¥Ê≥•ÊïôÂ†Ç„ÄÇ‰∏¶Âú®ÁéâÁ•ûËçâÂâµÁ∂ìË≤ªÊãÆÊçÆÁöÑÂàùÊúüÔºåËàáÈªÉÂΩ∞ËºùÈô¢Èï∑Á©çÊ•µÂêëÊôÆ‰∏ñÊïôÊúÉÁ±åÊé™Á∂ìË≤ªÔºå‰øÉÊàêÁéâÂ±±Á•ûÂ≠∏Èô¢ÁöÑÂâµÁ´ã„ÄÇ‰ªñ‰πüÈï∑ÊúüÈÄèÈÅéÂè∞ÁÅ£Âü∫Áù£Èï∑ËÄÅÊïôÊúÉÁöÑË≥áÊ∫êÔºåÂçîÂä©Â¶ªÂ≠êÂ≠´ÁêÜËìÆÂú®Âè∞ÁöÑÁ§æÊúÉÁ¶èÂà©Â∑•‰ΩúÔºåÂ¶ÇÂè∞ÁÅ£Êù±ÈÉ®ÂèäË•øÈÉ®ÁöÑÂ±±ÂçÄÈñãÂ±ïÈÜ´ÁôÇÂíåÊïëÊøüÁöÑÂ∑•‰Ωú„ÄÇÂ≠´ÈõÖÂêÑÁ∏ΩÊòØÈÅãÁî®ÂêÑÁ®ÆÁÆ°ÈÅìÁà≠ÂèñÊïôËÇ≤Ë≥áÊ∫êÂèäÂêà‰ΩúÂ∞çË±°ÔºåÂ¶ÇÂú®Êà∞ÂæåÂ§ßÈáèËΩâÈÄ≤Âè∞ÁÅ£ÁöÑÂ∑ÆÊúÉ‰∫∫ÂäõËàáË≤°ÂäõÂΩ¢Êàê„ÄåÂúãË™ûÊïôÊúÉ„ÄçÊÄ•ÈÄüÊì¥Â±ïÁöÑÁãÄÊ≥ÅÔºå‰ªñ‰πüÂú®‰∏≠ÈñìÊâÆÊºîÈõôÊñπÁöÑÊΩ§ÊªëÂäëÂíåÊï¥ÂêàÂäõÈáè„ÄÇ

      1967Âπ¥Â≠´ÈõÖÂêÑÂú®ÁæéÂúãÊ™¢È©óÂá∫ËÖ¶Áò§ÔºåÈö®Âç≥ËøîÂõûÊïÖÈÑâÂè∞ÁÅ£ÔºåÂêåÂπ¥6Êúà‰∏≠ÁóÖÈÄùÂ∂∫È†≠ÂÆ∂‰∏≠ÔºåËë¨ÊñºÂè∞ÁÅ£Á•ûÂ≠∏Èô¢„ÄÇËÄåÂ≠´ÁêÜËìÆÁπºÊâø‰ªñÁöÑÈÅ∫ÂøóÔºåÂú®ÈöîÂπ¥ÊàêÁ´ãÁÑöÊ£òÊµ∑Â§ñÂÆ£ÈÅìÊúÉÔºåÈ¶ñÂ∫¶Ê¥æÈÅ£Âè∞ÁÅ£Âéü‰ΩèÊ∞ëËá≥ÂçóÊ¥ãÈ¶¨‰æÜË•ø‰∫ûÁ†ÇÊãâË∂äÁ≠âÂú∞ÂÆ£Êïô„ÄÇÔºàÂè∞ÁÅ£Âü∫Áù£Èï∑ËÄÅÊïéÊúÉÁ∏ΩÊúÉÂéü‰ΩèÊ∞ëÂÆ£ÈÅìÂßîÂì°ÊúÉÔºå1998Ôºå297Ôºâ

      Rev. James Ira Dickson (Â≠´ÈõÖÂêÑ, 1900.2.23 ‚Äì 1967.6.15)

      James Ira Dickson was anAmerican Presbyterian missionary, theologian, and educator who played apivotal role in theological education and indigenous missions in Taiwan. His work significantly impacted theTaiwan Theological Seminary, indigenous church development, and social welfare initiatives.

      Early Life and Arrival in Taiwan (1927-1940)

      Born inSouth Dakota, USA,Dickson graduated from Princeton Theological Seminary in 1927. That same year, he marriedLillian Dickson (Â≠´ÁêÜËìÆ).

      During this period, theUnited Church of Canada movement causeddivisions within the Canadian Presbyterian Church, leading toa shortage of missionaries. Although he was American,Dickson was deeply influenced by the Student Volunteer Movement for Foreign Missions. InOctober 1927, he accepted amissionary appointment from the Presbyterian Church in Canada and was sent toTaiwan.

      In Taiwan, Dickson served invarious leadership roles:

      -Acting Principal of Tamsui Middle School (Ê∑°Ê∞¥‰∏≠Â≠∏)

      -President of Taipei Theological Seminary (now Taiwan Theological Seminary, Âè∞ÁÅ£Á•ûÂ≠∏Èô¢)

      -President of the Presbyterian Church in Taiwan Bible College

      However,in 1940, during thefinal years of World War II,the Japanese Governor-General‚Äôs Office pressured American missionaries to leave Taiwan. The Dickson family relocated toBritish Guiana (Guyana), South America, where they continuedmissionary work and theological education (Deng Xiangyang, 2003: 82).

      Post-War Return and Indigenous Missions (1946-1960s)

      After World War II, the Dicksonsreturned to Taiwan in 1946. That same year, James Dicksontraveled to Shanghai to invite Mennonite medical missionaries to serve Taiwan‚Äôs indigenous communities. As a result, in1947, theMennonite Central Committee (MCC) dispatched doctors and nurses, forming theMennonite Mountain Medical Team (Huang Wenjiang, Zhang Yunkai, Chen Zhiheng, 2015: 346).

      In1948, Dickson resumed his role asPresident of Taiwan Theological Seminary, where he led significant institutional changes. His mostcontroversial yet impactful decision was torelocate the seminary from Taipei‚Äôs Shuanglian district to Lingtou, Yangmingshan in1955, despite opposition. Over the nextten years, he oversaw the development of the new campus, completing the facilities by1965, before retiring.

      Church Renewal and Indigenous Evangelism

      Beyond theological education, Dickson was akey figure in Taiwan‚Äôs church renewal movement, particularly among young Presbyterian leaders in northern Taiwan.

      Hismost renowned contribution was hisdedication to indigenous missionary work.

      • InJune 1946, he visitedHualien‚Äôs mountain churches, marking the beginning ofhis deep engagement with indigenous evangelism.

        • Over the years, throughfundraising from local and international sources, he helpedbuild or renovate 385 churches, transforming many frombamboo structures to stone and concrete buildings.

          Establishment of Yushan Theological Seminary (ÁéâÂ±±Á•ûÂ≠∏Èô¢)

          During theearly years of Yushan Theological Seminary,financial constraints threatened its survival.Dickson and President Huang Zhanghui (ÈªÉÂΩ∞Ëºù) activelysecured international funding, ultimately ensuring the seminary‚Äôs establishment as a center forindigenous theological education.

          Support for Social Welfare and Medical Missions

          ThroughPresbyterian Church resources, Dicksonsupported his wife, Lillian Dickson, in hersocial welfare work, including:

          -Medical and relief programs in Taiwan‚Äôs mountain regions

          -Expansion of healthcare access in indigenous communities

          During thepost-war period, asurge in foreign missionaries and resources led to therapid growth of Mandarin-speaking churches (ÂúãË™ûÊïôÊúÉ). Dickson played acrucial mediating role, helpingintegrate foreign mission efforts with local church initiatives.

          Final Years and Legacy

          In1967, while in the United States,Dickson was diagnosed with a brain tumor. Heimmediately returned to Taiwan, where hepassed away in June at his home in Lingtou. He was buried atTaiwan Theological Seminary, where he had devoted much of his life to theological education.

          After his passing,Lillian Dickson carried on his mission work. In1968, she founded theBurning Bush Mission (ÁÑöÊ£òÊµ∑Â§ñÂÆ£ÈÅìÊúÉ), which sentTaiwanese indigenous missionaries toSarawak, Malaysia, marking the firstoverseas indigenous mission initiative from Taiwan (Indigenous Missions Committee of the Presbyterian Church in Taiwan, 1998: 297).

          Conclusion

          James Dickson was atransformational leader in Taiwan‚Äôs Christian history. Hispioneering work in theological education, indigenous evangelism, and church renewal left alasting legacy. Through hisinstitution-building, missionary efforts, and advocacy for indigenous ministry, he helpedshape Taiwan‚Äôs Presbyterian Church and strengthen its global connections.

          Ê•äÁæéÈΩãÁâßÂ∏´1949Âπ¥‰æÜÂà∞Âè∞ÁÅ£È¶ñÂÖàÂª∫Á´ãÂü∫Áù£ÊïôÊµ∏‰ø°ÊúÉ‰ªÅÊÑõÂ†ÇÔºåÁÇ∫Êµ∏‰ø°ÊúÉÂú®Âè∞ÁÅ£Á¨¨‰∏ÄÊâÄÊïôÊúÉÔºåÊ•äÁæéÈΩãÁâßÂ∏´Âú®ÁâßÊúÉÊúüÈñìÁúãË¶ãÂè∞ÁÅ£Á¶èÈü≥Â∑•‰ΩúÁöÑÈúÄË¶ÅÔºå‰∏¶Á©çÊ•µÈñãÊãìÂª∫Á´ãÁ¶èÈü≥‰∏≠ÂøÉÔºåÂ¶Ç‰ªäÂùáÂ∑≤ÊàêÁÇ∫Êµ∏‰ø°ÊúÉÊ≠∑Âè≤ÊÇ†‰πÖÁöÑÊïôÊúÉÔºåÂ¶ÇÊñ∞Á´πÊ®πÊûóÈ†≠ÈñãÂßãÂÆ∂Â∫≠ËÅöÊúÉÔºà1951Âπ¥12Êúà16Êó•Êñ∞Á´πÊµ∏‰ø°ÊúÉÊàêÁ´ãÔºâ„ÄÅÂªàÈñÄË°óÁ¶èÈü≥ÊâÄ„ÄÇ1952Âπ¥ÔºåÁæéÂçóÊµ∏‰ø°ÊúÉÂ∑ÆÊúÉÂú®Ëá∫ÁÅ£ÈñãËæ¶Âü∫Áù£ÊïôÂè∞ÁÅ£Êµ∏ÊúÉÁ•ûÂ≠∏Èô¢ÔºåÊüØÁêÜÂüπÁâßÂ∏´(Charles L. Culpepper, 1895-1986)ÁÇ∫Á¨¨‰∏Ä‰ªªÈô¢Èï∑ÔºõÂàùÊúüÂ∏´Ë≥á‰∏çË∂≥ÊôÇÔºåÊ•äÁæéÈΩãÊäïÂÖ•ËàäÁ¥ÑËÅñÁ∂ìÊïôÊéàÂ∑•‰ΩúÔºåÂçîÂä©ÂüπËÇ≤Ëá∫ÁÅ£Áï∂Âú∞ÁöÑÂÇ≥Êïô‰∫∫Êâç„ÄÇÊüØÁêÜÂüπÈô¢Èï∑„ÄÅ1948Âπ¥10Êúà‰æÜÂè∞ÂÆ£ÊïôÁöÑÁæéÂçóÊµ∏‰ø°ÊúÉÊòé‰øäÂæ∑ÂÆ£ÊïôÂ£´ÔºàMiss Bertha SmithÔºâÂèäÊ•äÁæéÈΩãÁâßÂ∏´‰∏â‰ΩçÂç≥ÁÇ∫Êó©ÊúüËá∫ÁÅ£Êµ∏‰ø°ÊúÉÈáçË¶ÅÊ≠∑Âè≤‰∫∫Áâ©„ÄÇÔºàÂê≥Êñá‰ªÅÔºå1998Ôºå49„ÄÅ50„ÄÅ233Ôºâ

          1961Âπ¥2Êúà23Êó•Ê•äÁæéÈΩãÂõ†ËÇùÁóÖÁôº‰ΩúÈÅé‰∏ñÔºåËë¨ÊñºÊñ∞ÂåóÂ∏ÇÊñ∞Â∫óÁ¢ßÊΩ≠Â±±ÂçÄ„ÄÇÊüØÁêÜÂüπÁâßÂ∏´Âú®ÂÖ∂ÂÆâÊÅØÁ¶ÆÊãúËá¥Ë©û‰∏≠Â∞äÁ®±‰ªñÁÇ∫„Äå‰∏ÄÁîüÂø†ÂøÉ‰∫ã‰∏ªÁöÑÂÉï‰∫∫„Äç„ÄÇ

          Rev. Vang Mei-Chai (Ê•äÁæéÈΩã, 1889.9.18 ‚Äì 1961.2.23)

          Rev.Vang Mei-Chai was aChinese Baptist missionary and church planter, recognized as thepioneer of Baptist ministry in Taiwan. His efforts were instrumental inestablishing the Baptist Church in Taiwan andtraining local Christian leaders.

          Early Life and Ministry in China (1889-1948)

          Born inMaguo Village, Shandong, China,Vang Mei-Chai received atraditional education in village schools before entering thePingdu Baptist Mission Middle School at age25. In1913, he enrolled at theBaptist Theological Seminary in Huang County, Shandong, graduating in1916.

          After graduation, heserved as a missionary inNortheastern China and Shandong. Beginning in1920, he spenttwelve years establishingtwelve churches acrossHarbin, Anda, Lindian, Mingshui, Ang‚Äôangxi, Qiqihar, Fulaerji, Zalantun, Jiamusi, Suifenhe, Boketu, and Manzhouli. His pioneering efforts helpedexpand Baptist mission work in Manchuria.

          In1936, at theCentennial Celebration of Baptist Missions in China in Guangzhou, he became an advocate forfrontier evangelism. The following year, during theFirst National Evangelistic Conference,21 pastors committed to frontier missions, includingVang Mei-Chai.

          In1948, theChinese Baptist Convention convened afrontier missions conference in Shanghai, where it was decided tosend Rev. Vang Mei-Chai to Taiwan to begin Baptist work. After aone-month exploratory trip, he returned to Shanghai and receivedofficial approval to startBaptist missions in Taiwan.

          Pioneering Baptist Missions in Taiwan (1949-1961)

          OnFebruary 4, 1949,Vang Mei-Chai arrived in Taiwan, landing inKeelung with his family. Over the next13 years, helaid the foundation for the Baptist Church in Taiwan, earning him the title‚ÄùPioneer of Baptist Ministry in Taiwan‚Äù.

          -1949 ‚Äì Established theRen-Ai Baptist Church (‰ªÅÊÑõÂ†Ç), thefirst Baptist church in Taiwan.

          -1951 ‚Äì Initiatedhouse church gatherings inShulin, Hsinchu, which later developed intoHsinchu Baptist Church (established onDecember 16, 1951).

          -1951 ‚Äì Opened theXiamen Street Gospel Center in Taipei.

          -1952 ‚Äì Played a key role in the founding of theTaiwan Baptist Theological Seminary (Âè∞ÁÅ£Êµ∏‰ø°ÊúÉÁ•ûÂ≠∏Èô¢), established by theSouthern Baptist Mission in Taiwan.

          • The seminary‚Äôs first president wasRev. Charles L. Culpepper (ÊüØÁêÜÂüπ, 1895-1986).

            • Due to ashortage of faculty,Vang Mei-Chai served as aprofessor of Old Testament, training Taiwan‚Äôs early Baptist pastors.

              • Along withRev. Culpepper andMiss Bertha Smith (Êòé‰øäÂæ∑, arrived in 1948), he became one of thethree most influential early figures in Taiwan‚Äôs Baptist history (Wu Wenren, 1998: 49, 50, 233).

                Final Years and Legacy

                OnFebruary 23, 1961,Vang Mei-Chai passed away from liver disease and was buried in theBitan Mountain area of Xindian, New Taipei City.

                At hismemorial service,Rev. Charles Culpepper honored him with the words:

                üëâ‚ÄúA faithful servant of the Lord throughout his life.‚Äù

                Significance in Taiwan‚Äôs Christian History

                Rev. Vang Mei-Chai‚Äôs contributions toTaiwan‚Äôs Baptist movement were foundational. He:

                -Planted Taiwan‚Äôs first Baptist churches.

                -Trained local pastors and church leaders.

                -Strengthened theological education throughTaiwan Baptist Theological Seminary.

                Hisevangelistic zeal, leadership, and dedication helpedestablish a lasting Baptist presence in Taiwan, shaping thegrowth of Baptist Christianity on the island.

                Witness Lee (ÊùéÂ∏∏Âèó, 1905.9.3 ‚Äì 1997.6.9)

                Witness Lee was aChinese Christian leader, preacher, and theologian, known for his leadership in theLocal Church Movement (Âè¨ÊúÉ/Âú∞ÊñπÂè¨ÊúÉ), as well as forestablishing Living Stream Ministry (Ê∞¥ÊµÅËÅ∑‰∫ãÁ´ô). As thesuccessor to Watchman Nee (ÂÄ™ÊüùËÅ≤), he played akey role in expanding the movement across China, Taiwan, and the United States.

                Early Life and Ministry in China (1905-1949)

                Born inNorth China, Lee was raised in aChristian family, with his mother being a devoted believer. Hisearly education was sporadic, but he attendedAmerican Presbyterian Church‚Äôs Yiwen Business School (ÁæéÂåóÈï∑ËÄÅÊïôÊúÉÁõäÊñáÂïÜÂ∞à), where hecommitted to full-time ministry at the age of 19.

                After encounteringWatchman Nee (ÂÄ™ÊüùËÅ≤), Lee became deeply influenced by his teachings. He worked as amissionary in Yantai and Shanghai, establishingLocal Churches in Northern China, including in his hometown ofYantai, Shandong (Lee, 2005: 48-50).

                In1934, Lee moved toShanghai, where he beganworking closely with Watchman Nee. That same year, Neeentrusted him with leading the Shanghai Gospel Book Room (‰∏äÊµ∑Á¶èÈü≥Êõ∏Êàø), the publishing branch of the movement. Over the next15 years, he ministered inNanjing, Shanghai, South China, and beyond, planting and strengthening Local Churches across China (Lee, 2005: 181-182).

                By1949, as theChinese Communist Party took control of China, Watchman Nee and other leaders sentLee to Taiwan to continue the work (Lee, 2005: 227-230).

                Establishing the Local Church in Taiwan (1949-1962)

                Arriving in Taiwan with only350 believers, Lee oversawrapid church growth‚Äîwithinfive years, membershipgrew to 20,000, leading to the global expansion of theLocal Church Movement.

                Key developments during this period included:

                -Founding the Taiwan Gospel Book Room (Âè∞ÁÅ£Á¶èÈü≥Êõ∏Êàø) to publish Christian literature.

                • ExpandingLocal Churches (Âú∞ÊñπÂè¨ÊúÉ) throughout Taiwan.

                  Doctrinal Disputes and Church Splits (1957-1965)

                  In1957, Lee had atheological disagreement withTheodore Austin-Sparks (Âè≤ÁôæÂÖã, 1888-1971), a leader of theInner Life Movement, regardingchurch structure and authority. This led to asplit in the Local Church in Taiwan (Lee, 2005: 32).

                  Further challenges to his leadership emerged:

                  -1961 ‚Äì TheManila Local Church split, questioning his authority.

                  -1965 ‚Äì Disputes overLee‚Äôs leadership led to furthercontroversies in Hong Kong (Lee, 2005: 84-88).

                  Expansion to the United States and Global Influence (1962-1997)

                  In1962, Lee moved toLos Angeles, USA, launching a35-year evangelical mission.

                  -1965 ‚Äì He foundedLiving Stream Ministry (Ê∞¥ÊµÅËÅ∑‰∫ãÁ´ô) inAnaheim, California, becoming the official publishing house forNee and Lee‚Äôs teachings.

                  -1965-1987 ‚Äì Leespent 22 years writing Life-Study of the Bible (ÁîüÂëΩËÆÄÁ∂ì), acomprehensive biblical commentary spanning 25,000+ pages in 89 volumes and 1,984 messages.

                  -1987 ‚Äì He completed theChinese Recovery Version of the New Testament (ÊÅ¢Âæ©Êú¨ËÅñÁ∂ì) and supervised itsEnglish translation.

                  Later Years and Controversies (1987-1997)

                  In his later years, Leereturned to Taiwan to promote the‚ÄùNew Way‚Äù (Êñ∞Ë∑ØÈÅãÂãï, also called ‚ÄúGod‚Äôs Ordained Way‚Äù/Á•ûÂëΩÂÆö‰πãË∑Ø), aiming torestructure the church and institutionalize leadership training. However, this movement facedresistance, particularly regardinghis handling of moral issues involving his son, Lee Meng-ze (ÊùéËíôÊæ§).

                  Despite these challenges, Lee continuedpreaching and writing. Even at91 years old (February 1997), he delivered public sermons atspecial conferences.

                  OnJune 9, 1997,Witness Lee passed away in Anaheim, California, at the age of 92.

                  Legacy

                  Lee left a lasting impact onChinese Christianity, particularly through:

                  1.The Global Expansion of the Local Church Movement

                  2.The Establishment of Living Stream Ministry

                  3.His Extensive Biblical Commentaries and Doctrinal Teachings

                  Despitetheological controversies and organizational disputes, his work continues toshape evangelical movements in China, Taiwan, and beyond.

    Lillian Dickson (Â≠´ÁêÜËìÆ, 1901.1.29 ‚Äì 1983.1.14)

    Lillian R. Dickson was anAmerican missionary, social worker, and humanitarian who played a key role inChristian missionary work and social welfare development in Taiwan, particularly amongindigenous communities, leprosy patients, and those in need of medical care and vocational training.

    Born onJanuary 29, 1901, inPelican Lake, Minnesota, USA, she attendedMacalester College. After graduating in 1924, her future husband,James Dickson, continued his studies atPrinceton Theological Seminary, while Lillian worked as a high school teacher before enrolling at theNew York Bible Theological Seminary (Liu Huihua, 1987).

    In1927, the newlywedJames and Lillian Dickson were sent asmissionaries by the Presbyterian Church in Canada. They traveled across the Pacific by ship, landing in Shanghai before making their way toKeelung, Taiwan, where they began theirmissionary journey‚Äîahoneymoon that became a lifelong calling (Su Guangyang, 1978).

    Early Years in Taiwan (1927-1941)

    The couple settled inTamsui in1929, whereRev. James Dickson served asprincipal of Tamsui Middle School and laterpresident of Taiwan Theological Seminary. He alsopreached among indigenous communities in the mountains.

    During this period, Lillian primarily took on therole of a wife and mother, raising their three children. However,World War II forced theDickson family to leave Taiwan due to their American nationality. They were reassigned toBritish Guiana (Guyana), South America, where they continued missionary work, gaining experience inSunday school programs and outdoor evangelism‚Äîpreparing Lillian for herreturn to Taiwan after the war (Tank, 1986).

    Post-War Return and Missionary Expansion (1947-1950s)

    InApril 1947, James and Lillian Dickson returned to Taiwan, with Jamesfocusing on theological education andindigenous evangelism. He spentweekdays teaching at the seminary andweekends driving a jeep into remote mountain areas to spread the gospel.Lillian accompanied him, dedicating herself towomen‚Äôs and children‚Äôs ministries (Li Zhende, 2005: 98, 111).

    She once told her husband:

    üëâ‚ÄúI don‚Äôt want to be just a missionary‚Äôs wife; I want to be a missionary wife.‚Äù

    To which he replied:

    üëâ‚ÄúYou can do things informally that the rest of us cannot.‚Äù

    During this period,many indigenous churches in Taiwan were established, largely due to the couple‚Äôsmissionary efforts. However, Lillian sawan even greater need in Taiwanese society and sooninitiated her own ministries (Su Guangyang, 1978).

    Social Welfare and Medical Ministries (1951-1960s)

    -1951 ‚Äì Lillian began publishingmonthly newsletters to update supporters in Canada and the U.S. She signed them with‚ÄùYours in His service‚Äù, emphasizing that donors werepartners in the ministry rather than mere financial contributors (Dickson, 2016). At her peak, she sent5,000 airmail and 20,000 regular mail letters per month.

    -1952 ‚Äì Responding to localpastors‚Äô calls for help, she beganministering at Lo-Sheng Leprosarium (Ê®ÇÁîüÁôÇÈ§äÈô¢) in Xinzhuang,providing medical, spiritual, and social care for leprosy patients. That year, shehelped establish Sheng-Wang Church (ËÅñÊúõÊïôÊúÉ) inside the leprosarium and continuedworshiping with patients until her death (Liu Huihua, 1987).

    -1954 ‚Äì She formally founded theMustard Seed Mission (Ëä•ËèúÁ®ÆÊúÉ), now known as theMustard Seed Christian Mission Foundation, tosupport social welfare efforts, funded primarily bydonors from the U.S. and Canada (Liu Huihua, 1987).

    -1956 ‚Äì Concerned abouttuberculosis among indigenous children, she establishedadult and children‚Äôs TB sanatoriums, which later led to the founding ofPuli Christian Hospital.

    -1960 ‚Äì InBeimen Township, Tainan, she established the‚ÄùGate of Mercy‚Äù clinic forblackfoot disease patients. TheMustard Seed Mission also providedmilk, rice, flour, and clothing for those in need (Dickson, 2016).

    -1961 ‚Äì She establishedmaternity wards for indigenous mothers, starting inHualien, and later expanded them across Taiwan. These maternity centers helpeddeliver approximately 240 babies per month until1975 (Tank, 1986).

    Vocational Training and Church Building (1958-1970s)

    -1958 ‚Äì TheMustard Seed Mission established vocational training centers in Hualien for indigenous youth, training over20,000 graduates in various trades. Nearlyhalf of the early indigenous pastors in thePresbyterian Church in Taiwan were alumni of these programs (Huang Wu-dong, 1954).

    -1963 ‚Äì She launched an initiative encouragingindigenous Christians to build stone churches, usinglocally sourced stone whilecement was funded by North American donors (Tank, 1986).

    International Missions and Final Years (1967-1983)

    -1967 ‚Äì At age66, Lillianexpanded her mission work overseas, founding theBurning Bush Mission (ÁÑöÊ£òÊµ∑Â§ñÂÆ£ÈÅìÊúÉ). She partnered with thePresbyterian Church in Taiwan, sendingeight indigenous pastors toSarawak, Malaysia, for mission work.

    Lillian Dicksonpassed away on January 14, 1983, after51 years of missionary, charitable, and educational service in Taiwan. She diedwhile actively working and wasburied alongside her husband at Taiwan Theological Seminary inLingtou, Taipei (Su Guangyang, 1978).

    Legacy

    Lillian Dickson wasone of the most influential missionaries in Taiwan‚Äôs history. Her workbridged evangelism and social action, addressingthe physical, educational, and spiritual needs of Taiwanese communities‚Äîespeciallyindigenous groups, leprosy patients, and the impoverished. Through herMustard Seed Mission, sheempowered thousands throughmedical care, education, and vocational training, shapingTaiwan‚Äôs Christian welfare landscape for generations.

      ËÄåÂ≠´ÈõÖÂêÑÊúÄÁÇ∫Áü•ÂêçÁöÑÔºåÂ∞±ÊòØÂú®ÈáçËøîÂè∞ÁÅ£ÂæåÊé®ÂãïÂè∞ÁÅ£Âéü‰ΩèÊ∞ëÂÆ£ÊïôÔºå1946Âπ¥6Êúà‰ªñÂ∞±Âà∞Ëä±ËìÆÂ∑°Ë¶ñÂ±±Âú∞ÊïôÊúÉÔºåÈñãÂßã‰ªñÁöÑÂéü‰ΩèÊ∞ëÂÆ£ÊïôÂ∑•‰Ωú„ÄÇÊï∏Âπ¥Èñì‰ªñÈÄèÈÅéÂúãÂÖßÂ§ñÁ±åÊ¨æÔºåÂçîÂä©‰∫Ü385ÊâÄÊïôÊúÉÂæûÁ´πÂª∫ÊïôÂ†ÇÊîπÂª∫ÊàñÊñ∞Âª∫ÁÇ∫Áü≥È†≠Ê∞¥Ê≥•ÊïôÂ†Ç„ÄÇ‰∏¶Âú®ÁéâÁ•ûËçâÂâµÁ∂ìË≤ªÊãÆÊçÆÁöÑÂàùÊúüÔºåËàáÈªÉÂΩ∞ËºùÈô¢Èï∑Á©çÊ•µÂêëÊôÆ‰∏ñÊïôÊúÉÁ±åÊé™Á∂ìË≤ªÔºå‰øÉÊàêÁéâÂ±±Á•ûÂ≠∏Èô¢ÁöÑÂâµÁ´ã„ÄÇ‰ªñ‰πüÈï∑ÊúüÈÄèÈÅéÂè∞ÁÅ£Âü∫Áù£Èï∑ËÄÅÊïôÊúÉÁöÑË≥áÊ∫êÔºåÂçîÂä©Â¶ªÂ≠êÂ≠´ÁêÜËìÆÂú®Âè∞ÁöÑÁ§æÊúÉÁ¶èÂà©Â∑•‰ΩúÔºåÂ¶ÇÂè∞ÁÅ£Êù±ÈÉ®ÂèäË•øÈÉ®ÁöÑÂ±±ÂçÄÈñãÂ±ïÈÜ´ÁôÇÂíåÊïëÊøüÁöÑÂ∑•‰Ωú„ÄÇÂ≠´ÈõÖÂêÑÁ∏ΩÊòØÈÅãÁî®ÂêÑÁ®ÆÁÆ°ÈÅìÁà≠ÂèñÊïôËÇ≤Ë≥áÊ∫êÂèäÂêà‰ΩúÂ∞çË±°ÔºåÂ¶ÇÂú®Êà∞ÂæåÂ§ßÈáèËΩâÈÄ≤Âè∞ÁÅ£ÁöÑÂ∑ÆÊúÉ‰∫∫ÂäõËàáË≤°ÂäõÂΩ¢Êàê„ÄåÂúãË™ûÊïôÊúÉ„ÄçÊÄ•ÈÄüÊì¥Â±ïÁöÑÁãÄÊ≥ÅÔºå‰ªñ‰πüÂú®‰∏≠ÈñìÊâÆÊºîÈõôÊñπÁöÑÊΩ§ÊªëÂäëÂíåÊï¥ÂêàÂäõÈáè„ÄÇ

      1967Âπ¥Â≠´ÈõÖÂêÑÂú®ÁæéÂúãÊ™¢È©óÂá∫ËÖ¶Áò§ÔºåÈö®Âç≥ËøîÂõûÊïÖÈÑâÂè∞ÁÅ£ÔºåÂêåÂπ¥6Êúà‰∏≠ÁóÖÈÄùÂ∂∫È†≠ÂÆ∂‰∏≠ÔºåËë¨ÊñºÂè∞ÁÅ£Á•ûÂ≠∏Èô¢„ÄÇËÄåÂ≠´ÁêÜËìÆÁπºÊâø‰ªñÁöÑÈÅ∫ÂøóÔºåÂú®ÈöîÂπ¥ÊàêÁ´ãÁÑöÊ£òÊµ∑Â§ñÂÆ£ÈÅìÊúÉÔºåÈ¶ñÂ∫¶Ê¥æÈÅ£Âè∞ÁÅ£Âéü‰ΩèÊ∞ëËá≥ÂçóÊ¥ãÈ¶¨‰æÜË•ø‰∫ûÁ†ÇÊãâË∂äÁ≠âÂú∞ÂÆ£Êïô„ÄÇÔºàÂè∞ÁÅ£Âü∫Áù£Èï∑ËÄÅÊïéÊúÉÁ∏ΩÊúÉÂéü‰ΩèÊ∞ëÂÆ£ÈÅìÂßîÂì°ÊúÉÔºå1998Ôºå297Ôºâ

      Rev. James Ira Dickson (Â≠´ÈõÖÂêÑ, 1900.2.23 ‚Äì 1967.6.15)

      James Ira Dickson was anAmerican Presbyterian missionary, theologian, and educator who played apivotal role in theological education and indigenous missions in Taiwan. His work significantly impacted theTaiwan Theological Seminary, indigenous church development, and social welfare initiatives.

      Early Life and Arrival in Taiwan (1927-1940)

      Born inSouth Dakota, USA,Dickson graduated from Princeton Theological Seminary in 1927. That same year, he marriedLillian Dickson (Â≠´ÁêÜËìÆ).

      During this period, theUnited Church of Canada movement causeddivisions within the Canadian Presbyterian Church, leading toa shortage of missionaries. Although he was American,Dickson was deeply influenced by the Student Volunteer Movement for Foreign Missions. InOctober 1927, he accepted amissionary appointment from the Presbyterian Church in Canada and was sent toTaiwan.

      In Taiwan, Dickson served invarious leadership roles:

      -Acting Principal of Tamsui Middle School (Ê∑°Ê∞¥‰∏≠Â≠∏)

      -President of Taipei Theological Seminary (now Taiwan Theological Seminary, Âè∞ÁÅ£Á•ûÂ≠∏Èô¢)

      -President of the Presbyterian Church in Taiwan Bible College

      However,in 1940, during thefinal years of World War II,the Japanese Governor-General‚Äôs Office pressured American missionaries to leave Taiwan. The Dickson family relocated toBritish Guiana (Guyana), South America, where they continuedmissionary work and theological education (Deng Xiangyang, 2003: 82).

      Post-War Return and Indigenous Missions (1946-1960s)

      After World War II, the Dicksonsreturned to Taiwan in 1946. That same year, James Dicksontraveled to Shanghai to invite Mennonite medical missionaries to serve Taiwan‚Äôs indigenous communities. As a result, in1947, theMennonite Central Committee (MCC) dispatched doctors and nurses, forming theMennonite Mountain Medical Team (Huang Wenjiang, Zhang Yunkai, Chen Zhiheng, 2015: 346).

      In1948, Dickson resumed his role asPresident of Taiwan Theological Seminary, where he led significant institutional changes. His mostcontroversial yet impactful decision was torelocate the seminary from Taipei‚Äôs Shuanglian district to Lingtou, Yangmingshan in1955, despite opposition. Over the nextten years, he oversaw the development of the new campus, completing the facilities by1965, before retiring.

      Church Renewal and Indigenous Evangelism

      Beyond theological education, Dickson was akey figure in Taiwan‚Äôs church renewal movement, particularly among young Presbyterian leaders in northern Taiwan.

      Hismost renowned contribution was hisdedication to indigenous missionary work.

      • InJune 1946, he visitedHualien‚Äôs mountain churches, marking the beginning ofhis deep engagement with indigenous evangelism.

        • Over the years, throughfundraising from local and international sources, he helpedbuild or renovate 385 churches, transforming many frombamboo structures to stone and concrete buildings.

          Establishment of Yushan Theological Seminary (ÁéâÂ±±Á•ûÂ≠∏Èô¢)

          During theearly years of Yushan Theological Seminary,financial constraints threatened its survival.Dickson and President Huang Zhanghui (ÈªÉÂΩ∞Ëºù) activelysecured international funding, ultimately ensuring the seminary‚Äôs establishment as a center forindigenous theological education.

          Support for Social Welfare and Medical Missions

          ThroughPresbyterian Church resources, Dicksonsupported his wife, Lillian Dickson, in hersocial welfare work, including:

          -Medical and relief programs in Taiwan‚Äôs mountain regions

          -Expansion of healthcare access in indigenous communities

          During thepost-war period, asurge in foreign missionaries and resources led to therapid growth of Mandarin-speaking churches (ÂúãË™ûÊïôÊúÉ). Dickson played acrucial mediating role, helpingintegrate foreign mission efforts with local church initiatives.

          Final Years and Legacy

          In1967, while in the United States,Dickson was diagnosed with a brain tumor. Heimmediately returned to Taiwan, where hepassed away in June at his home in Lingtou. He was buried atTaiwan Theological Seminary, where he had devoted much of his life to theological education.

          After his passing,Lillian Dickson carried on his mission work. In1968, she founded theBurning Bush Mission (ÁÑöÊ£òÊµ∑Â§ñÂÆ£ÈÅìÊúÉ), which sentTaiwanese indigenous missionaries toSarawak, Malaysia, marking the firstoverseas indigenous mission initiative from Taiwan (Indigenous Missions Committee of the Presbyterian Church in Taiwan, 1998: 297).

          Conclusion

          James Dickson was atransformational leader in Taiwan‚Äôs Christian history. Hispioneering work in theological education, indigenous evangelism, and church renewal left alasting legacy. Through hisinstitution-building, missionary efforts, and advocacy for indigenous ministry, he helpedshape Taiwan‚Äôs Presbyterian Church and strengthen its global connections.

          Ê•äÁæéÈΩãÁâßÂ∏´1949Âπ¥‰æÜÂà∞Âè∞ÁÅ£È¶ñÂÖàÂª∫Á´ãÂü∫Áù£ÊïôÊµ∏‰ø°ÊúÉ‰ªÅÊÑõÂ†ÇÔºåÁÇ∫Êµ∏‰ø°ÊúÉÂú®Âè∞ÁÅ£Á¨¨‰∏ÄÊâÄÊïôÊúÉÔºåÊ•äÁæéÈΩãÁâßÂ∏´Âú®ÁâßÊúÉÊúüÈñìÁúãË¶ãÂè∞ÁÅ£Á¶èÈü≥Â∑•‰ΩúÁöÑÈúÄË¶ÅÔºå‰∏¶Á©çÊ•µÈñãÊãìÂª∫Á´ãÁ¶èÈü≥‰∏≠ÂøÉÔºåÂ¶Ç‰ªäÂùáÂ∑≤ÊàêÁÇ∫Êµ∏‰ø°ÊúÉÊ≠∑Âè≤ÊÇ†‰πÖÁöÑÊïôÊúÉÔºåÂ¶ÇÊñ∞Á´πÊ®πÊûóÈ†≠ÈñãÂßãÂÆ∂Â∫≠ËÅöÊúÉÔºà1951Âπ¥12Êúà16Êó•Êñ∞Á´πÊµ∏‰ø°ÊúÉÊàêÁ´ãÔºâ„ÄÅÂªàÈñÄË°óÁ¶èÈü≥ÊâÄ„ÄÇ1952Âπ¥ÔºåÁæéÂçóÊµ∏‰ø°ÊúÉÂ∑ÆÊúÉÂú®Ëá∫ÁÅ£ÈñãËæ¶Âü∫Áù£ÊïôÂè∞ÁÅ£Êµ∏ÊúÉÁ•ûÂ≠∏Èô¢ÔºåÊüØÁêÜÂüπÁâßÂ∏´(Charles L. Culpepper, 1895-1986)ÁÇ∫Á¨¨‰∏Ä‰ªªÈô¢Èï∑ÔºõÂàùÊúüÂ∏´Ë≥á‰∏çË∂≥ÊôÇÔºåÊ•äÁæéÈΩãÊäïÂÖ•ËàäÁ¥ÑËÅñÁ∂ìÊïôÊéàÂ∑•‰ΩúÔºåÂçîÂä©ÂüπËÇ≤Ëá∫ÁÅ£Áï∂Âú∞ÁöÑÂÇ≥Êïô‰∫∫Êâç„ÄÇÊüØÁêÜÂüπÈô¢Èï∑„ÄÅ1948Âπ¥10Êúà‰æÜÂè∞ÂÆ£ÊïôÁöÑÁæéÂçóÊµ∏‰ø°ÊúÉÊòé‰øäÂæ∑ÂÆ£ÊïôÂ£´ÔºàMiss Bertha SmithÔºâÂèäÊ•äÁæéÈΩãÁâßÂ∏´‰∏â‰ΩçÂç≥ÁÇ∫Êó©ÊúüËá∫ÁÅ£Êµ∏‰ø°ÊúÉÈáçË¶ÅÊ≠∑Âè≤‰∫∫Áâ©„ÄÇÔºàÂê≥Êñá‰ªÅÔºå1998Ôºå49„ÄÅ50„ÄÅ233Ôºâ

          1961Âπ¥2Êúà23Êó•Ê•äÁæéÈΩãÂõ†ËÇùÁóÖÁôº‰ΩúÈÅé‰∏ñÔºåËë¨ÊñºÊñ∞ÂåóÂ∏ÇÊñ∞Â∫óÁ¢ßÊΩ≠Â±±ÂçÄ„ÄÇÊüØÁêÜÂüπÁâßÂ∏´Âú®ÂÖ∂ÂÆâÊÅØÁ¶ÆÊãúËá¥Ë©û‰∏≠Â∞äÁ®±‰ªñÁÇ∫„Äå‰∏ÄÁîüÂø†ÂøÉ‰∫ã‰∏ªÁöÑÂÉï‰∫∫„Äç„ÄÇ

          Rev. Vang Mei-Chai (Ê•äÁæéÈΩã, 1889.9.18 ‚Äì 1961.2.23)

          Rev.Vang Mei-Chai was aChinese Baptist missionary and church planter, recognized as thepioneer of Baptist ministry in Taiwan. His efforts were instrumental inestablishing the Baptist Church in Taiwan andtraining local Christian leaders.

          Early Life and Ministry in China (1889-1948)

          Born inMaguo Village, Shandong, China,Vang Mei-Chai received atraditional education in village schools before entering thePingdu Baptist Mission Middle School at age25. In1913, he enrolled at theBaptist Theological Seminary in Huang County, Shandong, graduating in1916.

          After graduation, heserved as a missionary inNortheastern China and Shandong. Beginning in1920, he spenttwelve years establishingtwelve churches acrossHarbin, Anda, Lindian, Mingshui, Ang‚Äôangxi, Qiqihar, Fulaerji, Zalantun, Jiamusi, Suifenhe, Boketu, and Manzhouli. His pioneering efforts helpedexpand Baptist mission work in Manchuria.

          In1936, at theCentennial Celebration of Baptist Missions in China in Guangzhou, he became an advocate forfrontier evangelism. The following year, during theFirst National Evangelistic Conference,21 pastors committed to frontier missions, includingVang Mei-Chai.

          In1948, theChinese Baptist Convention convened afrontier missions conference in Shanghai, where it was decided tosend Rev. Vang Mei-Chai to Taiwan to begin Baptist work. After aone-month exploratory trip, he returned to Shanghai and receivedofficial approval to startBaptist missions in Taiwan.

          Pioneering Baptist Missions in Taiwan (1949-1961)

          OnFebruary 4, 1949,Vang Mei-Chai arrived in Taiwan, landing inKeelung with his family. Over the next13 years, helaid the foundation for the Baptist Church in Taiwan, earning him the title‚ÄùPioneer of Baptist Ministry in Taiwan‚Äù.

          -1949 ‚Äì Established theRen-Ai Baptist Church (‰ªÅÊÑõÂ†Ç), thefirst Baptist church in Taiwan.

          -1951 ‚Äì Initiatedhouse church gatherings inShulin, Hsinchu, which later developed intoHsinchu Baptist Church (established onDecember 16, 1951).

          -1951 ‚Äì Opened theXiamen Street Gospel Center in Taipei.

          -1952 ‚Äì Played a key role in the founding of theTaiwan Baptist Theological Seminary (Âè∞ÁÅ£Êµ∏‰ø°ÊúÉÁ•ûÂ≠∏Èô¢), established by theSouthern Baptist Mission in Taiwan.

          • The seminary‚Äôs first president wasRev. Charles L. Culpepper (ÊüØÁêÜÂüπ, 1895-1986).

            • Due to ashortage of faculty,Vang Mei-Chai served as aprofessor of Old Testament, training Taiwan‚Äôs early Baptist pastors.

              • Along withRev. Culpepper andMiss Bertha Smith (Êòé‰øäÂæ∑, arrived in 1948), he became one of thethree most influential early figures in Taiwan‚Äôs Baptist history (Wu Wenren, 1998: 49, 50, 233).

                Final Years and Legacy

                OnFebruary 23, 1961,Vang Mei-Chai passed away from liver disease and was buried in theBitan Mountain area of Xindian, New Taipei City.

                At hismemorial service,Rev. Charles Culpepper honored him with the words:

                üëâ‚ÄúA faithful servant of the Lord throughout his life.‚Äù

                Significance in Taiwan‚Äôs Christian History

                Rev. Vang Mei-Chai‚Äôs contributions toTaiwan‚Äôs Baptist movement were foundational. He:

                -Planted Taiwan‚Äôs first Baptist churches.

                -Trained local pastors and church leaders.

                -Strengthened theological education throughTaiwan Baptist Theological Seminary.

                Hisevangelistic zeal, leadership, and dedication helpedestablish a lasting Baptist presence in Taiwan, shaping thegrowth of Baptist Christianity on the island.

                Witness Lee (ÊùéÂ∏∏Âèó, 1905.9.3 ‚Äì 1997.6.9)

                Witness Lee was aChinese Christian leader, preacher, and theologian, known for his leadership in theLocal Church Movement (Âè¨ÊúÉ/Âú∞ÊñπÂè¨ÊúÉ), as well as forestablishing Living Stream Ministry (Ê∞¥ÊµÅËÅ∑‰∫ãÁ´ô). As thesuccessor to Watchman Nee (ÂÄ™ÊüùËÅ≤), he played akey role in expanding the movement across China, Taiwan, and the United States.

                Early Life and Ministry in China (1905-1949)

                Born inNorth China, Lee was raised in aChristian family, with his mother being a devoted believer. Hisearly education was sporadic, but he attendedAmerican Presbyterian Church‚Äôs Yiwen Business School (ÁæéÂåóÈï∑ËÄÅÊïôÊúÉÁõäÊñáÂïÜÂ∞à), where hecommitted to full-time ministry at the age of 19.

                After encounteringWatchman Nee (ÂÄ™ÊüùËÅ≤), Lee became deeply influenced by his teachings. He worked as amissionary in Yantai and Shanghai, establishingLocal Churches in Northern China, including in his hometown ofYantai, Shandong (Lee, 2005: 48-50).

                In1934, Lee moved toShanghai, where he beganworking closely with Watchman Nee. That same year, Neeentrusted him with leading the Shanghai Gospel Book Room (‰∏äÊµ∑Á¶èÈü≥Êõ∏Êàø), the publishing branch of the movement. Over the next15 years, he ministered inNanjing, Shanghai, South China, and beyond, planting and strengthening Local Churches across China (Lee, 2005: 181-182).

                By1949, as theChinese Communist Party took control of China, Watchman Nee and other leaders sentLee to Taiwan to continue the work (Lee, 2005: 227-230).

                Establishing the Local Church in Taiwan (1949-1962)

                Arriving in Taiwan with only350 believers, Lee oversawrapid church growth‚Äîwithinfive years, membershipgrew to 20,000, leading to the global expansion of theLocal Church Movement.

                Key developments during this period included:

                -Founding the Taiwan Gospel Book Room (Âè∞ÁÅ£Á¶èÈü≥Êõ∏Êàø) to publish Christian literature.

                • ExpandingLocal Churches (Âú∞ÊñπÂè¨ÊúÉ) throughout Taiwan.

                  Doctrinal Disputes and Church Splits (1957-1965)

                  In1957, Lee had atheological disagreement withTheodore Austin-Sparks (Âè≤ÁôæÂÖã, 1888-1971), a leader of theInner Life Movement, regardingchurch structure and authority. This led to asplit in the Local Church in Taiwan (Lee, 2005: 32).

                  Further challenges to his leadership emerged:

                  -1961 ‚Äì TheManila Local Church split, questioning his authority.

                  -1965 ‚Äì Disputes overLee‚Äôs leadership led to furthercontroversies in Hong Kong (Lee, 2005: 84-88).

                  Expansion to the United States and Global Influence (1962-1997)

                  In1962, Lee moved toLos Angeles, USA, launching a35-year evangelical mission.

                  -1965 ‚Äì He foundedLiving Stream Ministry (Ê∞¥ÊµÅËÅ∑‰∫ãÁ´ô) inAnaheim, California, becoming the official publishing house forNee and Lee‚Äôs teachings.

                  -1965-1987 ‚Äì Leespent 22 years writing Life-Study of the Bible (ÁîüÂëΩËÆÄÁ∂ì), acomprehensive biblical commentary spanning 25,000+ pages in 89 volumes and 1,984 messages.

                  -1987 ‚Äì He completed theChinese Recovery Version of the New Testament (ÊÅ¢Âæ©Êú¨ËÅñÁ∂ì) and supervised itsEnglish translation.

                  Later Years and Controversies (1987-1997)

                  In his later years, Leereturned to Taiwan to promote the‚ÄùNew Way‚Äù (Êñ∞Ë∑ØÈÅãÂãï, also called ‚ÄúGod‚Äôs Ordained Way‚Äù/Á•ûÂëΩÂÆö‰πãË∑Ø), aiming torestructure the church and institutionalize leadership training. However, this movement facedresistance, particularly regardinghis handling of moral issues involving his son, Lee Meng-ze (ÊùéËíôÊæ§).

                  Despite these challenges, Lee continuedpreaching and writing. Even at91 years old (February 1997), he delivered public sermons atspecial conferences.

                  OnJune 9, 1997,Witness Lee passed away in Anaheim, California, at the age of 92.

                  Legacy

                  Lee left a lasting impact onChinese Christianity, particularly through:

                  1.The Global Expansion of the Local Church Movement

                  2.The Establishment of Living Stream Ministry

                  3.His Extensive Biblical Commentaries and Doctrinal Teachings

                  Despitetheological controversies and organizational disputes, his work continues toshape evangelical movements in China, Taiwan, and beyond.

    Lillian Dickson (Â≠´ÁêÜËìÆ, 1901.1.29 ‚Äì 1983.1.14)

    Lillian R. Dickson was anAmerican missionary, social worker, and humanitarian who played a key role inChristian missionary work and social welfare development in Taiwan, particularly amongindigenous communities, leprosy patients, and those in need of medical care and vocational training.

    Born onJanuary 29, 1901, inPelican Lake, Minnesota, USA, she attendedMacalester College. After graduating in 1924, her future husband,James Dickson, continued his studies atPrinceton Theological Seminary, while Lillian worked as a high school teacher before enrolling at theNew York Bible Theological Seminary (Liu Huihua, 1987).

    In1927, the newlywedJames and Lillian Dickson were sent asmissionaries by the Presbyterian Church in Canada. They traveled across the Pacific by ship, landing in Shanghai before making their way toKeelung, Taiwan, where they began theirmissionary journey‚Äîahoneymoon that became a lifelong calling (Su Guangyang, 1978).

    Early Years in Taiwan (1927-1941)

    The couple settled inTamsui in1929, whereRev. James Dickson served asprincipal of Tamsui Middle School and laterpresident of Taiwan Theological Seminary. He alsopreached among indigenous communities in the mountains.

    During this period, Lillian primarily took on therole of a wife and mother, raising their three children. However,World War II forced theDickson family to leave Taiwan due to their American nationality. They were reassigned toBritish Guiana (Guyana), South America, where they continued missionary work, gaining experience inSunday school programs and outdoor evangelism‚Äîpreparing Lillian for herreturn to Taiwan after the war (Tank, 1986).

    Post-War Return and Missionary Expansion (1947-1950s)

    InApril 1947, James and Lillian Dickson returned to Taiwan, with Jamesfocusing on theological education andindigenous evangelism. He spentweekdays teaching at the seminary andweekends driving a jeep into remote mountain areas to spread the gospel.Lillian accompanied him, dedicating herself towomen‚Äôs and children‚Äôs ministries (Li Zhende, 2005: 98, 111).

    She once told her husband:

    üëâ‚ÄúI don‚Äôt want to be just a missionary‚Äôs wife; I want to be a missionary wife.‚Äù

    To which he replied:

    üëâ‚ÄúYou can do things informally that the rest of us cannot.‚Äù

    During this period,many indigenous churches in Taiwan were established, largely due to the couple‚Äôsmissionary efforts. However, Lillian sawan even greater need in Taiwanese society and sooninitiated her own ministries (Su Guangyang, 1978).

    Social Welfare and Medical Ministries (1951-1960s)

    -1951 ‚Äì Lillian began publishingmonthly newsletters to update supporters in Canada and the U.S. She signed them with‚ÄùYours in His service‚Äù, emphasizing that donors werepartners in the ministry rather than mere financial contributors (Dickson, 2016). At her peak, she sent5,000 airmail and 20,000 regular mail letters per month.

    -1952 ‚Äì Responding to localpastors‚Äô calls for help, she beganministering at Lo-Sheng Leprosarium (Ê®ÇÁîüÁôÇÈ§äÈô¢) in Xinzhuang,providing medical, spiritual, and social care for leprosy patients. That year, shehelped establish Sheng-Wang Church (ËÅñÊúõÊïôÊúÉ) inside the leprosarium and continuedworshiping with patients until her death (Liu Huihua, 1987).

    -1954 ‚Äì She formally founded theMustard Seed Mission (Ëä•ËèúÁ®ÆÊúÉ), now known as theMustard Seed Christian Mission Foundation, tosupport social welfare efforts, funded primarily bydonors from the U.S. and Canada (Liu Huihua, 1987).

    -1956 ‚Äì Concerned abouttuberculosis among indigenous children, she establishedadult and children‚Äôs TB sanatoriums, which later led to the founding ofPuli Christian Hospital.

    -1960 ‚Äì InBeimen Township, Tainan, she established the‚ÄùGate of Mercy‚Äù clinic forblackfoot disease patients. TheMustard Seed Mission also providedmilk, rice, flour, and clothing for those in need (Dickson, 2016).

    -1961 ‚Äì She establishedmaternity wards for indigenous mothers, starting inHualien, and later expanded them across Taiwan. These maternity centers helpeddeliver approximately 240 babies per month until1975 (Tank, 1986).

    Vocational Training and Church Building (1958-1970s)

    -1958 ‚Äì TheMustard Seed Mission established vocational training centers in Hualien for indigenous youth, training over20,000 graduates in various trades. Nearlyhalf of the early indigenous pastors in thePresbyterian Church in Taiwan were alumni of these programs (Huang Wu-dong, 1954).

    -1963 ‚Äì She launched an initiative encouragingindigenous Christians to build stone churches, usinglocally sourced stone whilecement was funded by North American donors (Tank, 1986).

    International Missions and Final Years (1967-1983)

    -1967 ‚Äì At age66, Lillianexpanded her mission work overseas, founding theBurning Bush Mission (ÁÑöÊ£òÊµ∑Â§ñÂÆ£ÈÅìÊúÉ). She partnered with thePresbyterian Church in Taiwan, sendingeight indigenous pastors toSarawak, Malaysia, for mission work.

    Lillian Dicksonpassed away on January 14, 1983, after51 years of missionary, charitable, and educational service in Taiwan. She diedwhile actively working and wasburied alongside her husband at Taiwan Theological Seminary inLingtou, Taipei (Su Guangyang, 1978).

    Legacy

    Lillian Dickson wasone of the most influential missionaries in Taiwan‚Äôs history. Her workbridged evangelism and social action, addressingthe physical, educational, and spiritual needs of Taiwanese communities‚Äîespeciallyindigenous groups, leprosy patients, and the impoverished. Through herMustard Seed Mission, sheempowered thousands throughmedical care, education, and vocational training, shapingTaiwan‚Äôs Christian welfare landscape for generations.

      ËÄåÂ≠´ÈõÖÂêÑÊúÄÁÇ∫Áü•ÂêçÁöÑÔºåÂ∞±ÊòØÂú®ÈáçËøîÂè∞ÁÅ£ÂæåÊé®ÂãïÂè∞ÁÅ£Âéü‰ΩèÊ∞ëÂÆ£ÊïôÔºå1946Âπ¥6Êúà‰ªñÂ∞±Âà∞Ëä±ËìÆÂ∑°Ë¶ñÂ±±Âú∞ÊïôÊúÉÔºåÈñãÂßã‰ªñÁöÑÂéü‰ΩèÊ∞ëÂÆ£ÊïôÂ∑•‰Ωú„ÄÇÊï∏Âπ¥Èñì‰ªñÈÄèÈÅéÂúãÂÖßÂ§ñÁ±åÊ¨æÔºåÂçîÂä©‰∫Ü385ÊâÄÊïôÊúÉÂæûÁ´πÂª∫ÊïôÂ†ÇÊîπÂª∫ÊàñÊñ∞Âª∫ÁÇ∫Áü≥È†≠Ê∞¥Ê≥•ÊïôÂ†Ç„ÄÇ‰∏¶Âú®ÁéâÁ•ûËçâÂâµÁ∂ìË≤ªÊãÆÊçÆÁöÑÂàùÊúüÔºåËàáÈªÉÂΩ∞ËºùÈô¢Èï∑Á©çÊ•µÂêëÊôÆ‰∏ñÊïôÊúÉÁ±åÊé™Á∂ìË≤ªÔºå‰øÉÊàêÁéâÂ±±Á•ûÂ≠∏Èô¢ÁöÑÂâµÁ´ã„ÄÇ‰ªñ‰πüÈï∑ÊúüÈÄèÈÅéÂè∞ÁÅ£Âü∫Áù£Èï∑ËÄÅÊïôÊúÉÁöÑË≥áÊ∫êÔºåÂçîÂä©Â¶ªÂ≠êÂ≠´ÁêÜËìÆÂú®Âè∞ÁöÑÁ§æÊúÉÁ¶èÂà©Â∑•‰ΩúÔºåÂ¶ÇÂè∞ÁÅ£Êù±ÈÉ®ÂèäË•øÈÉ®ÁöÑÂ±±ÂçÄÈñãÂ±ïÈÜ´ÁôÇÂíåÊïëÊøüÁöÑÂ∑•‰Ωú„ÄÇÂ≠´ÈõÖÂêÑÁ∏ΩÊòØÈÅãÁî®ÂêÑÁ®ÆÁÆ°ÈÅìÁà≠ÂèñÊïôËÇ≤Ë≥áÊ∫êÂèäÂêà‰ΩúÂ∞çË±°ÔºåÂ¶ÇÂú®Êà∞ÂæåÂ§ßÈáèËΩâÈÄ≤Âè∞ÁÅ£ÁöÑÂ∑ÆÊúÉ‰∫∫ÂäõËàáË≤°ÂäõÂΩ¢Êàê„ÄåÂúãË™ûÊïôÊúÉ„ÄçÊÄ•ÈÄüÊì¥Â±ïÁöÑÁãÄÊ≥ÅÔºå‰ªñ‰πüÂú®‰∏≠ÈñìÊâÆÊºîÈõôÊñπÁöÑÊΩ§ÊªëÂäëÂíåÊï¥ÂêàÂäõÈáè„ÄÇ

      1967Âπ¥Â≠´ÈõÖÂêÑÂú®ÁæéÂúãÊ™¢È©óÂá∫ËÖ¶Áò§ÔºåÈö®Âç≥ËøîÂõûÊïÖÈÑâÂè∞ÁÅ£ÔºåÂêåÂπ¥6Êúà‰∏≠ÁóÖÈÄùÂ∂∫È†≠ÂÆ∂‰∏≠ÔºåËë¨ÊñºÂè∞ÁÅ£Á•ûÂ≠∏Èô¢„ÄÇËÄåÂ≠´ÁêÜËìÆÁπºÊâø‰ªñÁöÑÈÅ∫ÂøóÔºåÂú®ÈöîÂπ¥ÊàêÁ´ãÁÑöÊ£òÊµ∑Â§ñÂÆ£ÈÅìÊúÉÔºåÈ¶ñÂ∫¶Ê¥æÈÅ£Âè∞ÁÅ£Âéü‰ΩèÊ∞ëËá≥ÂçóÊ¥ãÈ¶¨‰æÜË•ø‰∫ûÁ†ÇÊãâË∂äÁ≠âÂú∞ÂÆ£Êïô„ÄÇÔºàÂè∞ÁÅ£Âü∫Áù£Èï∑ËÄÅÊïéÊúÉÁ∏ΩÊúÉÂéü‰ΩèÊ∞ëÂÆ£ÈÅìÂßîÂì°ÊúÉÔºå1998Ôºå297Ôºâ

      Rev. James Ira Dickson (Â≠´ÈõÖÂêÑ, 1900.2.23 ‚Äì 1967.6.15)

      James Ira Dickson was anAmerican Presbyterian missionary, theologian, and educator who played apivotal role in theological education and indigenous missions in Taiwan. His work significantly impacted theTaiwan Theological Seminary, indigenous church development, and social welfare initiatives.

      Early Life and Arrival in Taiwan (1927-1940)

      Born inSouth Dakota, USA,Dickson graduated from Princeton Theological Seminary in 1927. That same year, he marriedLillian Dickson (Â≠´ÁêÜËìÆ).

      During this period, theUnited Church of Canada movement causeddivisions within the Canadian Presbyterian Church, leading toa shortage of missionaries. Although he was American,Dickson was deeply influenced by the Student Volunteer Movement for Foreign Missions. InOctober 1927, he accepted amissionary appointment from the Presbyterian Church in Canada and was sent toTaiwan.

      In Taiwan, Dickson served invarious leadership roles:

      -Acting Principal of Tamsui Middle School (Ê∑°Ê∞¥‰∏≠Â≠∏)

      -President of Taipei Theological Seminary (now Taiwan Theological Seminary, Âè∞ÁÅ£Á•ûÂ≠∏Èô¢)

      -President of the Presbyterian Church in Taiwan Bible College

      However,in 1940, during thefinal years of World War II,the Japanese Governor-General‚Äôs Office pressured American missionaries to leave Taiwan. The Dickson family relocated toBritish Guiana (Guyana), South America, where they continuedmissionary work and theological education (Deng Xiangyang, 2003: 82).

      Post-War Return and Indigenous Missions (1946-1960s)

      After World War II, the Dicksonsreturned to Taiwan in 1946. That same year, James Dicksontraveled to Shanghai to invite Mennonite medical missionaries to serve Taiwan‚Äôs indigenous communities. As a result, in1947, theMennonite Central Committee (MCC) dispatched doctors and nurses, forming theMennonite Mountain Medical Team (Huang Wenjiang, Zhang Yunkai, Chen Zhiheng, 2015: 346).

      In1948, Dickson resumed his role asPresident of Taiwan Theological Seminary, where he led significant institutional changes. His mostcontroversial yet impactful decision was torelocate the seminary from Taipei‚Äôs Shuanglian district to Lingtou, Yangmingshan in1955, despite opposition. Over the nextten years, he oversaw the development of the new campus, completing the facilities by1965, before retiring.

      Church Renewal and Indigenous Evangelism

      Beyond theological education, Dickson was akey figure in Taiwan‚Äôs church renewal movement, particularly among young Presbyterian leaders in northern Taiwan.

      Hismost renowned contribution was hisdedication to indigenous missionary work.

      • InJune 1946, he visitedHualien‚Äôs mountain churches, marking the beginning ofhis deep engagement with indigenous evangelism.

        • Over the years, throughfundraising from local and international sources, he helpedbuild or renovate 385 churches, transforming many frombamboo structures to stone and concrete buildings.

          Establishment of Yushan Theological Seminary (ÁéâÂ±±Á•ûÂ≠∏Èô¢)

          During theearly years of Yushan Theological Seminary,financial constraints threatened its survival.Dickson and President Huang Zhanghui (ÈªÉÂΩ∞Ëºù) activelysecured international funding, ultimately ensuring the seminary‚Äôs establishment as a center forindigenous theological education.

          Support for Social Welfare and Medical Missions

          ThroughPresbyterian Church resources, Dicksonsupported his wife, Lillian Dickson, in hersocial welfare work, including:

          -Medical and relief programs in Taiwan‚Äôs mountain regions

          -Expansion of healthcare access in indigenous communities

          During thepost-war period, asurge in foreign missionaries and resources led to therapid growth of Mandarin-speaking churches (ÂúãË™ûÊïôÊúÉ). Dickson played acrucial mediating role, helpingintegrate foreign mission efforts with local church initiatives.

          Final Years and Legacy

          In1967, while in the United States,Dickson was diagnosed with a brain tumor. Heimmediately returned to Taiwan, where hepassed away in June at his home in Lingtou. He was buried atTaiwan Theological Seminary, where he had devoted much of his life to theological education.

          After his passing,Lillian Dickson carried on his mission work. In1968, she founded theBurning Bush Mission (ÁÑöÊ£òÊµ∑Â§ñÂÆ£ÈÅìÊúÉ), which sentTaiwanese indigenous missionaries toSarawak, Malaysia, marking the firstoverseas indigenous mission initiative from Taiwan (Indigenous Missions Committee of the Presbyterian Church in Taiwan, 1998: 297).

          Conclusion

          James Dickson was atransformational leader in Taiwan‚Äôs Christian history. Hispioneering work in theological education, indigenous evangelism, and church renewal left alasting legacy. Through hisinstitution-building, missionary efforts, and advocacy for indigenous ministry, he helpedshape Taiwan‚Äôs Presbyterian Church and strengthen its global connections.
